data-uotrack=\"InlineShareCommentsBtn\"><span class=\"inline-share-count inline-share-count-comments\">\n                \n                    2\n                \n            </span><span class=\"inline-share-btn-label inline-share-btn-label-comments\">COMMENT</span></span><span\n        class=\"inline-share-btn inline-share-btn-email\"\n        data-share-method=\"email\"\n        data-uotrack=\"InlineShareEmailBtn\"><span class=\"inline-share-count inline-share-count-email\"></span><span class=\"inline-share-btn-label inline-share-btn-label-email\">EMAIL</span></span><span\n         class=\"inline-share-btn inline-share-btn-more\"\n         data-share-method=\"facebook\"\n         data-uotrack=\"InlineShareMoreBtn\"><span class=\"inline-share-count inline-share-count-more\"></span><span class=\"inline-share-btn-label inline-share-btn-label-more\">MORE</span></span></div></div><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>WASHINGTON \u2014 Syria's\u00a0<a href=\"/story/news/world/2017/03/10/syria-civil-war-six-years-old-al-waer-homs/98998192/\">civil war\u00a0began six years ago </a>as a popular revolt against the brutal regime of President Bashar Assad.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Now a domestic uprising that has left <a href=\"/story/news/world/2016/12/13/aleppo-syria-civil-war/95370898/\">an estimated 500,000 dead and millions homeless</a> has morphed into a global war, sucking\u00a0major powers into a conflict growing\u00a0more volatile by the day.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>The United States, Russia, Turkey\u00a0and\u00a0Iran\u00a0\u2014 as well as\u00a0foreign terrorists \u2014 have joined the fray with an\u00a0array of forces, often with conflicting\u00a0objectives. Overhead, the airspace is crowded with Russian and coalition aircraft, sometimes flying within sight of one another, as they pursue separate air campaigns.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>In the shadows, Saudi Arabia and other countries in the Middle East have secretly backed armed fighters\u00a0to overthrow Assad.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><strong>More on Syria:</strong></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><a href=\"/story/news/politics/2017/04/06/us-launches-cruise-missile-strike-syria-after-chemical-weapons-attack/100142330/\">U.S. launches cruise missile strike on Syria after chemical weapons attack</a></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><a href=\"/story/news/world/2017/04/06/us-launches-strikes-against-syria-heres-what-we-know/100148690/\">U.S. launches strikes against Syria: Here's what we know</a></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><a href=\"/story/news/world/2017/03/14/syria-civil-war-russia-united-states-turkey-iran/99002088/\">Syria's civil war: A look at how we got to this point</a></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><a href=\"/story/news/politics/2017/04/06/trump-syria-red-line-what-trump-has-said/100147916/\">President Trump finds his line-in-the-sand moment</a></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Rivalries and alliances are constantly shifting.\u00a0\u201cIt's the most complicated environment and situation I've been in in my life,\u201d said Army Lt. Gen. Stephen Townsend, the commander of coalition forces in Iraq and Syria.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Here is how one country's civil war turned into an international crisis:</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><strong>THE START</strong></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>The powder keg was lit in March 2011, when a group of boys were tortured by Assad\u2019s agents for having written graffiti supporting the Arab Spring, a string of popular uprisings against autocratic regimes throughout the Arab world.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Thousands protested the boys' treatment, and\u00a0the regime responded by killing dozens. Revolts against Assad, a member of the minority Alawite sect, began to spread, as some of his\u00a0officers defected to opposition forces.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>The Obama administration called for Assad to step down because of his brutal crackdown against dissidents, but it did not support the rebels at a time when it could have made a difference, said Mustafa Alani, director of national security for the Gulf Research Center in Geneva.\u00a0\u201cThere was no clear decisive attitude from the Obama administration.\u201d</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><div cxenseparse_start --><p>Iran and Russia, by contrast, did not hesitate to throw support behind their long-time ally, Assad. Even so, the rebellion spread, and Assad\u00a0lost control of large\u00a0swaths of territory, particularly in the north.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><strong>ISIS IS BORN</strong></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>The growing chaos\u00a0attracted terrorists, including remnants of al-Qaeda in Iraq. An al-Qaeda offshoot that called\u00a0itself the Islamic State,\u00a0ISIL or\u00a0ISIS\u00a0grew\u00a0in power as it battled Assad\u2019s forces.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>By 2014, the Islamic State began seizing terrain, including Raqqa and the oil region Deir ez-Zor. ISIS\u00a0emerged\u00a0as the most powerful of the forces battling\u00a0Assad.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Later that year, the militant group launched a lightning strike into Iraq, capturing Mosul, the country\u2019s second-largest city, and nearly made it to the outskirts of Baghdad. The attack caught Iraq and the rest of the world\u00a0by surprise.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><div id=\"module-position-P21I1lbTYXE\" class=\"story-asset image-asset\"><aside itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"wide single-photo\"><img itemprop=\"url\" src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/1128b755faffb66329af623c600fc2d73fc54ef0/c=130-0-2160-1526&amp;r=x408&amp;c=540x405/local/-/media/2017/03/13/USATODAY/USATODAY/636250003895294443-AFP-AFP-MM29F.jpg\" alt=\"This file photo taken on December 7, 2016 shows general\" width=\"540\" height=\"405\" data-mycapture-src=\"\"\n                    data-mycapture-sm-src=\"\" /><meta itemprop=\"name\" content=\"AFP AFP_MM29F I CFG SYR\" /><meta itemprop=\"height\" content=\"405\" /><meta itemprop=\"width\" content=\"540\" /><p>This file photo taken on December 7, 2016 shows general view of destruction in the al-Shaar neighborhood after government forces took control of the area in the eastern part of Aleppo.<meta itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\" content=\"George Ourfalian, AFP/Getty Images\" />&nbsp;<span class=\"credit\">(Photo: George Ourfalian, AFP/Getty Images)</span></p></aside><div class=\"clearfix\"></div></div><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><strong>U.S. FORCES ENTER</strong></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>In August 2014, the United States responded with a bombing campaign against Islamic State\u00a0targets in Iraq\u00a0to blunt the militants' expansion. A month later, U.S. warplanes\u00a0began striking targets in Syria, where the militants were headquartered.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Today,\u00a0<a href=\"/story/news/world/2016/09/28/united-states-military-presence-iraq/91220510/\">5,000 U.S. troops are in Iraq</a> to train and advise Iraqi forces. Another 500 Americans in Syria support\u00a0a coalition of 45,000 Arab and Kurdish forces battling the Islamic State.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Airstrikes by a U.S.-led\u00a0coalition have helped local ground forces drive ISIS\u00a0out of a number of towns in northeastern Syria and are\u00a0now intensifying efforts on Raqqa, the militants' headquarters.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><div id=\"module-position-P21I1lhsr3Y\" class=\"story-asset oembed-asset\"><div class=\"story-oembed-wrap \"><div \n        class=\"js-oembed story-oembed story-oembed-usa-today story-oembed-type-link\"\n        data-oembed-type=\"link\"\n        data-oembed-provider=\"usa-today\"><style>\n    .oembed-asset-link { border-bottom: 1px solid #e1e1e1; }\n    .oembed-link-anchor { display: block; clear: both; }\n    p.oembed-link-desc { font-size: 100%; color: #666; font-weight: normal; margin: 0 14px 14px 14px; font-family: 'Futura Today Light'; text-align: left; line-height: 120%; }\n</style><div class=\"oembed-asset oembed-asset-link oembed-asset-usa-today oembed-simple-link-container\"><a href=\"http://www.usatoday.com/story/news/world/2017/01/17/united-states-planes-dropping-supplies-isil-syria/96558314/\" data-track-label=\"newrecircinline\" class=\"oembed-simple-link oembed-link-anchor\"><p class=\"oembed-link-desc\">U.S. increasing airdrops of supplies to forces battling ISIL in Syria</p><div style=\"clear: both;\"></div></a></div></div></div></div><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><strong>RUSSIA, IRAN SAVE ASSAD</strong></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>As the Islamic State has lost ground, Assad has strengthened his position. Assad\u00a0appeared to be on the verge of defeat until Russia entered the battle in September 2015.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><div id=\"module-position-P21I1lgi19Y\" class=\"story-asset image-asset\"><aside itemprop=\"associatedMedia\" itemscope itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ImageObject\" class=\"wide single-photo\"><img itemprop=\"url\" src=\"https://www.gannett-cdn.com/-mm-/977b462c8f46f9124fc521004770d17999296d29/c=0-172-1981-1661&amp;r=x408&amp;c=540x405/local/-/media/2017/03/10/USATODAY/USATODAY/636247406889171291-AP-Syria.jpg\" alt=\"Syrian President Bashar Assad speaks during an interview\" width=\"540\" height=\"405\" data-mycapture-src=\"\"\n                    data-mycapture-sm-src=\"\" /><meta itemprop=\"name\" content=\"AP SYRIA I SYR\" /><meta itemprop=\"height\" content=\"405\" /><meta itemprop=\"width\" content=\"540\" /><p>Syrian President Bashar Assad speaks during an interview with Yahoo News in Damascus, Syria, on Feb. 10, 2017. Assad said, there are &quot;definitely&quot; terrorists among the millions of Syrians seeking refuge in the West.<meta itemprop=\"copyrightHolder\" content=\"AP\" />&nbsp;<span class=\"credit\">(Photo: AP)</span></p></aside><div class=\"clearfix\"></div></div><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>When the Russians first started bombing,\u00a0Assad was losing territory. \u201cThe Russian intervention stopped the potential collapse of the regime and it led to a not insignificant recapture of territory,\u201d said Chris Chivvis,\u00a0associate director of the international security and defense policy center at Rand Corp.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>While Russia dropped bombs, Iran\u00a0provided critical ground forces that\u00a0included\u00a0Hezbollah, an Iranian-backed\u00a0militia based in Lebanon.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Last year, <a href=\"/story/news/world/2016/12/14/syria-aleppo-humanitarian-crisis-not-genocide/95423636/\">Russian-backed Syrian forces seized Aleppo</a>, once the country\u2019s largest city and a rebel stronghold. That battle was a major victory for Assad, and a huge loss for the prospect\u00a0that a\u00a0moderate opposition would\u00a0come to power.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><div id=\"module-position-P21I1lhdAJg\" class=\"story-asset oembed-asset\"><div class=\"story-oembed-wrap \"><div \n        class=\"js-oembed story-oembed story-oembed-usa-today story-oembed-type-link\"\n        data-oembed-type=\"link\"\n        data-oembed-provider=\"usa-today\"><style>\n    .oembed-asset-link { border-bottom: 1px solid #e1e1e1; }\n    .oembed-link-anchor { display: block; clear: both; }\n    p.oembed-link-desc { font-size: 100%; color: #666; font-weight: normal; margin: 0 14px 14px 14px; font-family: 'Futura Today Light'; text-align: left; line-height: 120%; }\n</style><div class=\"oembed-asset oembed-asset-link oembed-asset-usa-today oembed-simple-link-container\"><a href=\"http://www.usatoday.com/story/news/world/2016/12/14/syria-aleppo-humanitarian-crisis-not-genocide/95423636/\" data-track-label=\"newrecircinline\" class=\"oembed-simple-link oembed-link-anchor\"><p class=\"oembed-link-desc\">Aleppo killings are humanitarian crisis, but not &#39;genocide&#39;</p><div style=\"clear: both;\"></div></a></div></div></div></div><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>\u201cThat really marked the destruction of the last bastion of acceptable opposition groups in northern Syria,\u201d said Christopher Kozak, an analyst at the Institute for the Study of War.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Today, most of the remaining opposition to Assad is made up of radical\u00a0groups, including the Islamic State and al-Qaeda affiliates.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><strong>TURKS VS.\u00a0KURDS\u00a0</strong></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Turkey, once one of Assad\u2019s fiercest opponents and a key U.S. ally in the region, is increasingly focused on what it sees as a greater threat: Kurdish fighters who may try to seize parts of Syria, Iraq and Turkey for an independent enclave.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>The Kurds, who have tenacious ground forces,\u00a0have been one of America\u2019s most reliable partners\u00a0against the Islamic\u00a0State. When Iraq\u2019s army was still in disarray, it was the Kurds who were holding out against the militants'\u00a0onslaught.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>With U.S.\u00a0help, the Kurds have rolled up successes in much of northeast Syria, along the Turkish border.\u00a0But their battlefield prowess worries the Turkish government\u00a0that they will unite\u00a0with Kurdish separatist\u00a0groups inside Turkey.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Turkish\u00a0Prime Minister\u00a0Binali Yildirim\u00a0said the <a href=\"/story/news/world/2017/03/09/turkey-sees-threat-us-plan-raqqa/98976986/\">United States would damage its relations with Turkey</a> if it allows the Kurds to participate in the offensive to oust ISIS from\u00a0Raqqa.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Coalition commander Townsend said the\u00a0U.S. military is discussing with Turkey and the Kurds how both can help in the Raqqa campaign.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><strong>BATTLEFIELD GETS SMALLER</strong></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>As the Islamic State loses territory, the potential for mishaps among the foreign forces grows, raising worries that a mistake could lead to a wider war. Last month, for example, Russian aircraft accidentally struck U.S.-backed Syrian forces near Manbij, a town near the Turkish border. U.S. advisers were nearby but not hurt.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Last September U.S. aircraft mistakenly targeted Syrian regime forces in<strong>\u00a0</strong>Deir el-Zour, killing dozens. The Pentagon said it was a series of errors that led the U.S. military to believe they were targeting the Islamic State.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Sorting out all the forces on the ground is getting more difficult,\u00a0said\u00a0Gen. Herbert Carlisle, head of Air Combat Command.\u00a0\"As we continue to squeeze ISIS and continue to take more territory away from them, it becomes more complex.\"</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><div "https://www.usatoday.com/story/news/world/2017/03/14/syria-civil-war-russia-united-states-turkey-iran/99002088/", "date": "04-06-2017", "source": "usa-today", "text": "CLOSE Skip in Skip x Embed x Share Foreign affairs reporter Oren Dorell explains who is fighting whom in the Syrian Civil War in two minutes. USA TODAY\n\nA child carries manuals distributed by UNICEF volunteers in the area following an informative session on identifying and reporting an unexploded object, at Al- Sakhoor neighborhood, east Aleppo, Syria. (Photo: Khudr Al-Issa, AP)\n\nWASHINGTON \u2014 Syria's civil war began six years ago as a popular revolt against the brutal regime of President Bashar Assad.\n\nNow a domestic uprising that has left an estimated 500,000 dead and millions homeless has morphed into a global war, sucking major powers into a conflict growing more volatile by the day.\n\nThe United States, Russia, Turkey and Iran \u2014 as well as foreign terrorists \u2014 have joined the fray with an array of forces, often with conflicting objectives. Overhead, the airspace is crowded with Russian and coalition aircraft, sometimes flying within sight of one another, as they pursue separate air campaigns.\n\nIn the shadows, Saudi Arabia and other countries in the Middle East have secretly backed armed fighters to overthrow Assad.\n\nMore on Syria:\n\nU.S. launches cruise missile strike on Syria after chemical weapons attack\n\nU.S. launches strikes against Syria: Here's what we know\n\nSyria's civil war: A look at how we got to this point\n\nPresident Trump finds his line-in-the-sand moment\n\nRivalries and alliances are constantly shifting. \u201cIt's the most complicated environment and situation I've been in in my life,\u201d said Army Lt. Gen. Stephen Townsend, the commander of coalition forces in Iraq and Syria.\n\nHere is how one country's civil war turned into an international crisis:\n\nTHE START\n\nThe powder keg was lit in March 2011, when a group of boys were tortured by Assad\u2019s agents for having written graffiti supporting the Arab Spring, a string of popular uprisings against autocratic regimes throughout the Arab world.\n\nThousands protested the boys' treatment, and the regime responded by killing dozens. Revolts against Assad, a member of the minority Alawite sect, began to spread, as some of his officers defected to opposition forces.\n\nThe Obama administration called for Assad to step down because of his brutal crackdown against dissidents, but it did not support the rebels at a time when it could have made a difference, said Mustafa Alani, director of national security for the Gulf Research Center in Geneva. \u201cThere was no clear decisive attitude from the Obama administration.\u201d\n\nIran and Russia, by contrast, did not hesitate to throw support behind their long-time ally, Assad. Even so, the rebellion spread, and Assad lost control of large swaths of territory, particularly in the north.\n\nISIS IS BORN\n\nThe growing chaos attracted terrorists, including remnants of al-Qaeda in Iraq. An al-Qaeda offshoot that called itself the Islamic State, ISIL or ISIS grew in power as it battled Assad\u2019s forces.\n\nBy 2014, the Islamic State began seizing terrain, including Raqqa and the oil region Deir ez-Zor. ISIS emerged as the most powerful of the forces battling Assad.\n\nLater that year, the militant group launched a lightning strike into Iraq, capturing Mosul, the country\u2019s second-largest city, and nearly made it to the outskirts of Baghdad. The attack caught Iraq and the rest of the world by surprise.\n\nThis file photo taken on December 7, 2016 shows general view of destruction in the al-Shaar neighborhood after government forces took control of the area in the eastern part of Aleppo. (Photo: George Ourfalian, AFP/Getty Images)\n\nU.S. FORCES ENTER\n\nIn August 2014, the United States responded with a bombing campaign against Islamic State targets in Iraq to blunt the militants' expansion. A month later, U.S. warplanes began striking targets in Syria, where the militants were headquartered.\n\nToday, 5,000 U.S. troops are in Iraq to train and advise Iraqi forces. Another 500 Americans in Syria support a coalition of 45,000 Arab and Kurdish forces battling the Islamic State.\n\nAirstrikes by a U.S.-led coalition have helped local ground forces drive ISIS out of a number of towns in northeastern Syria and are now intensifying efforts on Raqqa, the militants' headquarters.\n\nRUSSIA, IRAN SAVE ASSAD\n\nAs the Islamic State has lost ground, Assad has strengthened his position. Assad appeared to be on the verge of defeat until Russia entered the battle in September 2015.\n\nSyrian President Bashar Assad speaks during an interview with Yahoo News in Damascus, Syria, on Feb. 10, 2017. Assad said, there are \"definitely\" terrorists among the millions of Syrians seeking refuge in the West. (Photo: AP)\n\nWhen the Russians first started bombing, Assad was losing territory. \u201cThe Russian intervention stopped the potential collapse of the regime and it led to a not insignificant recapture of territory,\u201d said Chris Chivvis, associate director of the international security and defense policy center at Rand Corp.\n\nWhile Russia dropped bombs, Iran provided critical ground forces that included Hezbollah, an Iranian-backed militia based in Lebanon.\n\nLast year, Russian-backed Syrian forces seized Aleppo, once the country\u2019s largest city and a rebel stronghold. That battle was a major victory for Assad, and a huge loss for the prospect that a moderate opposition would come to power.\n\n\u201cThat really marked the destruction of the last bastion of acceptable opposition groups in northern Syria,\u201d said Christopher Kozak, an analyst at the Institute for the Study of War.\n\nToday, most of the remaining opposition to Assad is made up of radical groups, including the Islamic State and al-Qaeda affiliates.\n\nTURKS VS. KURDS\n\nTurkey, once one of Assad\u2019s fiercest opponents and a key U.S. ally in the region, is increasingly focused on what it sees as a greater threat: Kurdish fighters who may try to seize parts of Syria, Iraq and Turkey for an independent enclave.\n\nThe Kurds, who have tenacious ground forces, have been one of America\u2019s most reliable partners against the Islamic State. When Iraq\u2019s army was still in disarray, it was the Kurds who were holding out against the militants' onslaught.\n\nWith U.S. help, the Kurds have rolled up successes in much of northeast Syria, along the Turkish border. But their battlefield prowess worries the Turkish government that they will unite with Kurdish separatist groups inside Turkey.\n\nTurkish Prime Minister Binali Yildirim said the United States would damage its relations with Turkey if it allows the Kurds to participate in the offensive to oust ISIS from Raqqa.\n\nCoalition commander Townsend said the U.S. military is discussing with Turkey and the Kurds how both can help in the Raqqa campaign.\n\nBATTLEFIELD GETS SMALLER\n\nAs the Islamic State loses territory, the potential for mishaps among the foreign forces grows, raising worries that a mistake could lead to a wider war. Last month, for example, Russian aircraft accidentally struck U.S.-backed Syrian forces near Manbij, a town near the Turkish border. U.S. advisers were nearby but not hurt.\n\nLast September U.S. aircraft mistakenly targeted Syrian regime forces in Deir el-Zour, killing dozens. The Pentagon said it was a series of errors that led the U.S. military to believe they were targeting the Islamic State.\n\nSorting out all the forces on the ground is getting more difficult, said Gen. Herbert Carlisle, head of Air Combat Command. \"As we continue to squeeze ISIS and continue to take more territory away from them, it becomes more complex.\"\n\nREAD MORE:\n\nRead or Share this story:
